<meta name="DC.AccessRights" content="open-access" />
<meta name="DC.Description" content="In multicellular organisms, exchange of genetic information occurs mainly among individuals belonging to the same species through sexual reproduction. Lateral gene transfer between distantly related taxa has been demonstrated in some cases, but its frequency and evolutionary importance have been controversial. By comparing genomes of many grasses, we show that large blocks of DNA containing functional genes are laterally passed among distantly related species. Some of these genes are then used by the recipient species, expanding their genetic toolkit. The spread of functional genes across grasses that have developed distinct physiological and ecological adaptations may therefore represent a significant evolutionary driving force in this globally important group of plants." />

<meta name="citation_title" content="Lateral transfers of large DNA fragments spread functional genes among grasses" />
<meta name="citation_abstract" lang="en" content="&lt;p&gt;A fundamental tenet of multicellular eukaryotic evolution is that vertical inheritance is paramount, with natural selection acting on genetic variants transferred from parents to offspring. This lineal process means that an organism’s adaptive potential can be restricted by its evolutionary history, the amount of standing genetic variation, and its mutation rate. Lateral gene transfer (LGT) theoretically provides a mechanism to bypass many of these limitations, but the evolutionary importance and frequency of this process in multicellular eukaryotes, such as plants, remains debated. We address this issue by assembling a chromosome-level genome for the grass &lt;i&gt;Alloteropsis semialata&lt;/i&gt;, a species surmised to exhibit two LGTs, and screen it for other grass-to-grass LGTs using genomic data from 146 other grass species. Through stringent phylogenomic analyses, we discovered 57 additional LGTs in the &lt;i&gt;A. semialata&lt;/i&gt; nuclear genome, involving at least nine different donor species. The LGTs are clustered in 23 laterally acquired genomic fragments that are up to 170 kb long and have accumulated during the diversification of &lt;i&gt;Alloteropsis.&lt;/i&gt; The majority of the 59 LGTs in &lt;i&gt;A. semialata&lt;/i&gt; are expressed, and we show that they have added functions to the recipient genome. Functional LGTs were further detected in the genomes of five other grass species, demonstrating that this process is likely widespread in this globally important group of plants. LGT therefore appears to represent a potent evolutionary force capable of spreading functional genes among distantly related grass species.&lt;/p&gt;" />
<meta name="citation_abstract" lang="en" scheme="executive-summary" content="&lt;h3&gt;Significance&lt;/h3&gt;
&lt;p&gt;In multicellular organisms, exchange of genetic information occurs mainly among individuals belonging to the same species through sexual reproduction. Lateral gene transfer between distantly related taxa has been demonstrated in some cases, but its frequency and evolutionary importance have been controversial. By comparing genomes of many grasses, we show that large blocks of DNA containing functional genes are laterally passed among distantly related species. Some of these genes are then used by the recipient species, expanding their genetic toolkit. The spread of functional genes across grasses that have developed distinct physiological and ecological adaptations may therefore represent a significant evolutionary driving force in this globally important group of plants.&lt;/p&gt;" />

<meta name="twitter:title" content="Lateral transfers of large DNA fragments spread functional genes among grasses" />
<meta name="twitter:card" content="summary_large_image" />
<meta name="twitter:image" content="https://www.pnas.org/content/116/10/4416/F1.large.jpg" />
<meta name="twitter:description" content="In multicellular organisms, exchange of genetic information occurs mainly among individuals belonging to the same species through sexual reproduction. Lateral gene transfer between distantly related taxa has been demonstrated in some cases, but its frequency and evolutionary importance have been controversial. By comparing genomes of many grasses, we show that large blocks of DNA containing functional genes are laterally passed among distantly related species. Some of these genes are then used by the recipient species, expanding their genetic toolkit. The spread of functional genes across grasses that have developed distinct physiological and ecological adaptations may therefore represent a significant evolutionary driving force in this globally important group of plants." />

<meta name="og-description" property="og:description" content="In multicellular organisms, exchange of genetic information occurs mainly among individuals belonging to the same species through sexual reproduction. Lateral gene transfer between distantly related taxa has been demonstrated in some cases, but its frequency and evolutionary importance have been controversial. By comparing genomes of many grasses, we show that large blocks of DNA containing functional genes are laterally passed among distantly related species. Some of these genes are then used by the recipient species, expanding their genetic toolkit. The spread of functional genes across grasses that have developed distinct physiological and ecological adaptations may therefore represent a significant evolutionary driving force in this globally important group of plants." />

  <div class="pane-content">
    <div class="highwire-article-citation highwire-citation-type-highwire-article" data-node-nid="852296" id="node852296" data-pisa="pnas;116/10/4416" data-pisa-master="pnas;1810031116" data-apath="/pnas/116/10/4416.atom" data-hw-author-tooltip-instance="highwire_author_tooltip"><div class="highwire-cite highwire-cite-highwire-article highwire-citation-pnas-article-title-complete clearfix has-author-tooltip highwire-citation-highwire-article-top-a">
  
  
      <div class="highwire-cite-overline"><span  class="highwire-cite-metadata-article-category highwire-cite-metadata">Research Article</span></div>
  
  
      <h1 class="highwire-cite-title" id="page-title">Lateral transfers of large DNA fragments spread functional genes among grasses</h1>  
    	<div class="highwire-cite-authors"><span  class="highwire-citation-authors"><span class="highwire-citation-author first" data-delta="0">Luke T. Dunning</span>, <span class="highwire-citation-author" data-delta="1">Jill K. <div class="panel-separator"></div><div class="panel-pane pane-highwire-panel-tabs-container" >
  
      
  
  <div class="pane-content">
    <div data-panels-ajax-tab-preloaded="jnl_pnas_tab_art" id="panels-ajax-tab-container-highwire_article_tabs" class="panels-ajax-tab-container"><div class="panels-ajax-tab-loading" style ="display:none"><img class="loading" src="https://www.pnas.org/sites/all/modules/contrib/panels_ajax_tab/images/loading.gif" alt="Loading" title="Loading" /></div><div class="panels-ajax-tab-wrap-jnl_pnas_tab_art"><div class="panel-display panel-1col clearfix" >
  <div class="panel-panel panel-col">
    <div><div class="panel-pane pane-highwire-markup" >
  
      
  
  <div class="pane-content">
    <div class="highwire-markup"><div xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml" id="content-block-markup" data-highwire-cite-ref-tooltip-instance="highwire_reflinks_tooltip" xmlns:xhtml="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml"><div class="article fulltext-view "><span class="highwire-journal-article-marker-start"></span><div class="executive-summary"><h2 class="">Significance</h2><p id="p-5">In multicellular organisms, exchange of genetic information occurs mainly among individuals belonging to the same species through sexual reproduction. Lateral gene transfer between distantly related taxa has been demonstrated in some cases, but its frequency and evolutionary importance have been controversial. By comparing genomes of many grasses, we show that large blocks of DNA containing functional genes are laterally passed among distantly related species. Some of these genes are then used by the recipient species, expanding their genetic toolkit. The spread of functional genes across grasses that have developed distinct physiological and ecological adaptations may therefore represent a significant evolutionary driving force in this globally important group of plants.</p></div><div class="section abstract" id="abstract-2"><h2>Abstract</h2><p id="p-6">A fundamental tenet of multicellular eukaryotic evolution is that vertical inheritance is paramount, with natural selection acting on genetic variants transferred from parents to offspring. This lineal process means that an organism’s adaptive potential can be restricted by its evolutionary history, the amount of standing genetic variation, and its mutation rate. Lateral gene transfer (LGT) theoretically provides a mechanism to bypass many of these limitations, but the evolutionary importance and frequency of this process in multicellular eukaryotes, such as plants, remains debated. We address this issue by assembling a chromosome-level genome for the grass <em>Alloteropsis semialata</em>, a species surmised to exhibit two LGTs, and screen it for other grass-to-grass LGTs using genomic data from 146 other grass species. Through stringent phylogenomic analyses, we discovered 57 additional LGTs in the <em>A. semialata</em> nuclear genome, involving at least nine different donor species. The LGTs are clustered in 23 laterally acquired genomic fragments that are up to 170 kb long and have accumulated during the diversification of <em>Alloteropsis.</em> The majority of the 59 LGTs in <em>A. semialata</em> are expressed, and we show that they have added functions to the recipient genome. Functional LGTs were further detected in the genomes of five other grass species, demonstrating that this process is likely widespread in this globally important group of plants. LGT therefore appears to represent a potent evolutionary force capable of spreading functional genes among distantly related grass species.</p></div><ul class="kwd-group"><li class="kwd"><a href="/keyword/adaptation" class="hw-term hw-article-keyword hw-article-keyword-adaptation" rel="nofollow">adaptation</a></li><li class="kwd"><a href="/keyword/genome" class="hw-term hw-article-keyword hw-article-keyword-genome" rel="nofollow">genome</a></li><li class="kwd"><a href="/keyword/poaceae" class="hw-term hw-article-keyword hw-article-keyword-poaceae" rel="nofollow">Poaceae</a></li><li class="kwd"><a href="/keyword/horizontal-gene-transfer" class="hw-term hw-article-keyword hw-article-keyword-horizontal-gene-transfer" rel="nofollow">horizontal gene transfer</a></li><li class="kwd"><a href="/keyword/phylogenetics" class="hw-term hw-article-keyword hw-article-keyword-phylogenetics" rel="nofollow">phylogenetics</a></li></ul><p id="p-7" class="flushleft">During evolution, organisms adapt to new or changing environments as a result of natural selection acting on genetic variation. In multicellular eukaryotes, this process is traditionally considered to concern mutations transferred from parents to offspring. The possibility of any given organism evolving novel traits can therefore be constrained by the genetic variants existing within an interbreeding population or species, and the rate of new mutations (<a id="xref-ref-1-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-1">1</a>). Therefore, a novel trait can take protracted evolutionary periods to develop, with incremental modifications per generation. Furthermore, divergent evolutionary histories may mean that particular traits are restricted to lineages that possess the appropriate genetic precursors (<a id="xref-ref-2-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-2">2</a>). The transfer of genes among distantly related species can theoretically allow organisms to bypass these limitations. However, the frequency of this phenomenon, and therefore its importance for the evolutionary diversification of multicellular eukaryotes, remains unclear (<a id="xref-ref-3-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-3">3</a><a id="xref-ref-4-1" class="xref-down-link" href="#ref-4"><span>⇓</span></a><a id="xref-ref-5-1" class="xref-down-link" href="#ref-5"><span>⇓</span></a><a id="xref-ref-6-1" class="xref-down-link" href="#ref-6"><span>⇓</span></a><a id="xref-ref-7-1" class="xref-down-link" href="#ref-7"><span>⇓</span></a><a id="xref-ref-8-1" class="xref-down-link" href="#ref-8"><span>⇓</span></a><a id="xref-ref-9-1" class="xref-down-link" href="#ref-9"><span>⇓</span></a>–<a id="xref-ref-10-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-10">10</a>).</p><p id="p-8">Lateral gene transfer (LGT) is the movement of genetic material between organisms belonging to distinct groups of interbreeding individuals, and therefore involves mechanisms other than classical sexual reproduction. Its pervasiveness is well documented in prokaryotes, where it can rapidly spread adaptive traits such as antibiotic resistance among distantly related taxa (<a id="xref-ref-11-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-11">11</a>). Reports of LGTs have also been accumulating for multiple groups of eukaryotes, frequently involving unicellular recipients or donors (e.g., refs. <a id="xref-ref-12-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-12">12</a><a id="xref-ref-13-1" class="xref-down-link" href="#ref-13"><span>⇓</span></a><a id="xref-ref-14-1" class="xref-down-link" href="#ref-14"><span>⇓</span></a>–<a id="xref-ref-15-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-15">15</a>). While LGTs have been less commonly reported among multicellular eukaryotes, convincing cases exist where genes of adaptive significance have been transferred (e.g., refs. <a id="xref-ref-3-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-3">3</a>, <a id="xref-ref-16-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-16">16</a>, and <a id="xref-ref-17-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-17">17</a>). Among plants, most known LGTs concern mitochondrial genes (<a id="xref-ref-18-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-18">18</a><a id="xref-ref-19-1" class="xref-down-link" href="#ref-19"><span>⇓</span></a><a id="xref-ref-20-1" class="xref-down-link" href="#ref-20"><span>⇓</span></a>–<a id="xref-ref-21-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-21">21</a>) and/or parasitic interactions (<a id="xref-ref-22-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-22">22</a><a id="xref-ref-23-1" class="xref-down-link" href="#ref-23"><span>⇓</span></a><a id="xref-ref-24-1" class="xref-down-link" href="#ref-24"><span>⇓</span></a><a id="xref-ref-25-1" class="xref-down-link" href="#ref-25"><span>⇓</span></a><a id="xref-ref-26-1" class="xref-down-link" href="#ref-26"><span>⇓</span></a><a id="xref-ref-27-1" class="xref-down-link" href="#ref-27"><span>⇓</span></a><a id="xref-ref-28-1" class="xref-down-link" href="#ref-28"><span>⇓</span></a><a id="xref-ref-29-1" class="xref-down-link" href="#ref-29"><span>⇓</span></a>–<a id="xref-ref-30-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-30">30</a>), with only a few nonparasitic plant-to-plant LGT of nuclear genes yet recorded (<a id="xref-ref-31-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-31">31</a><a id="xref-ref-32-1" class="xref-down-link" href="#ref-32"><span>⇓</span></a><a id="xref-ref-33-1" class="xref-down-link" href="#ref-33"><span>⇓</span></a>–<a id="xref-ref-34-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-34">34</a>). Genome scans suggest that transposable elements (TEs) are frequently transferred among plants (<a id="xref-ref-35-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-35">35</a>), but similar searches of laterally acquired coding genes are needed to assess the frequency and functional significance of nuclear LGT among nonparasitic plants.</p><p id="p-9">To determine the importance of LGTs for functional diversification in a group of nonparasitic plants, we quantified the prevalence, retention through time, and functional significance of LGT in the grass <em>Alloteropsis semialata</em> (tribe Paniceae in subfamily Panicoideae). This species is distributed throughout the paleotropics and exhibits geographic variation in the presence of two genes encoding key C<sub>4</sub> photosynthetic enzymes that were laterally acquired from distantly related grass species (<a id="xref-ref-32-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-32">32</a>, <a id="xref-ref-36-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-36">36</a>). The donor species diverged from <em>A. semialata</em> over 20 My ago (<a id="xref-ref-32-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-32">32</a>), more than ample time for the complete turnover of intergenic regions (<a id="xref-ref-37-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-37">37</a><a id="xref-ref-38-1" class="xref-down-link" href="#ref-38"><span>⇓</span></a>–<a id="xref-ref-39-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-39">39</a>). Classical introgression involving chromosome pairing and recombination is therefore unlikely (<a id="xref-ref-32-4" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-32">32</a>). There are no known parasitic grasses (<a id="xref-ref-40-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-40">40</a>), and <em>A. semialata</em> must therefore have received its additional genetic information via a different transfer mechanism than those reported for symbiotic partners (<a id="xref-ref-1-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-1">1</a>). One of the two LGTs previously detected in <em>A. semialata</em> appears to be restricted to Australia (<a id="xref-ref-36-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-36">36</a>), enabling comparisons between closely related individuals with and without it. The identification of the putative donor (<a id="xref-ref-32-5" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-32">32</a>), coupled with such recent LGTs, provides a tractable system to evaluate the evolutionary significance of grass-to-grass LGT.</p><p id="p-10">In this present work, we generate and assemble a chromosome-level genome for an Australian individual of <em>A. semialata</em> known to contain one LGT received from the distantly related Panicoideae grass <em>Themeda</em> (tribe Andropogoneae), and one received from a more closely related grass species belonging to a different subtribe within the Paniceae (<em>Cenchrus</em> sp. in the Cenchrinae) (<a id="xref-ref-32-6" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-32">32</a>, <a id="xref-ref-36-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-36">36</a>). Using genomic data from another 146 grasses, including members of the groups known to have donated genes to certain <em>Alloteropsis</em> populations (<a id="xref-ref-32-7" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-32">32</a>), we then adopt stringent phylogenomic approaches to first identify all unambiguous LGTs in the reference genome of <em>A. semialata</em> and to determine for each of them the putative donor. The genomic data are then used to determine the size and number of DNA blocks that were laterally acquired in <em>Alloteropsis</em>, as well as the transposable elements that appear to have accompanied them. We then use genomic data for conspecifics and congeners of the reference genome to determine when the genes were acquired during the diversification of <em>Alloteropsis</em>, testing the hypothesis that LGTs have gradually accumulated in the genome. Finally, gene expression data are used to determine whether the LGTs are transcribed and test the hypothesis that their acquisition added functional diversity to the recipient genome.</p><div class="section results" id="sec-1"><h2 class="">Results</h2><div id="sec-2" class="subsection"><h3>Assembly and Annotation of a Chromosome-Level Reference Genome.</h3><p id="p-11">We sequenced and assembled a 0.75-Gb chromosome-level reference genome for a single Australian individual of <em>A. semialata</em> (<a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1810031116/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, <em>Supplementary Methods</em> and Fig. S1</a>). In total, 97.5% of the genome assembly was contained in nine scaffolds, which corresponds to the expected number of chromosomes for this individual (2<em>n =</em> 18) (<a id="xref-ref-41-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-41">41</a>). Synteny is well conserved between the genome of <em>A. semialata</em> and those of other Panicoideae grasses, such as the Cenchrinae <em>Setaria italica</em> (<a id="xref-ref-42-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-42">42</a>) and, to a lesser extent, the Andropogoneae <em>Sorghum bicolor</em> (<a id="xref-ref-43-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-43">43</a>) (<a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1810031116/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Fig. S2</a>); this is as expected, given high overall synteny among grasses (<a id="xref-ref-44-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-44">44</a>, <a id="xref-ref-45-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-45">45</a>).</p><p id="p-12">The genome contains 22,043 high-confidence annotated protein-coding genes, with BLAST matches in both SwissProt and at least one of the two other Panicoideae genomes (<em>S. italica</em> or <em>S. bicolor</em>). The gene density was plotted across the genome in 1-Mb windows. Each chromosome has a region of reduced gene density, and these regions are assumed to correspond to centromeres (<a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1810031116/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Fig. S3<em>A</em></a>). All putative centromeres are located roughly in the middle of the chromosome, apart from chromosomes 7 and 9 which appear acrocentric (<a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1810031116/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Fig. S3<em>A</em></a>).</p></div><div id="sec-3" class="subsection"><h3>Phylogenomics Identifies Multiple LGTs in <em>A. semialata</em>.</h3><p id="p-13">We adopted a tiered approach to determine the proportion of the 22,043 high-confidence annotated genes within the genome that were laterally acquired from other plant species (<a id="xref-fig-1-1" class="xref-fig" href="#F1">Fig. 1</a> and <a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1810031116/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, <em>Supplementary Methods</em></a>). By specifically focusing on plant-to-plant transfers, we limit the risks of contamination and false positives associated with LGT studies involving microorganisms (<a id="xref-ref-4-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-4">4</a>, <a id="xref-ref-6-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-6">6</a>). We first performed BLAST searches against angiosperm genomes to determine whether any <em>A. semialata</em> gene is more similar to a nongrass angiosperm than to other grasses. No such evidence for gene transfer involving a nongrass angiosperm donor was found, and we therefore focused our searches on grass-to-grass LGTs. Our strategy used existing and novel genomic resources for 147 grass species in a pipeline combining similarity analyses with phylogenetic validation (<a id="xref-fig-1-2" class="xref-fig" href="#F1">Fig. 1</a>). The analytical approach is analogous to previous scans for LGT (e.g., refs. <a id="xref-ref-29-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-29">29</a> and <a id="xref-ref-32-8" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-32">32</a>), but extra validation steps were made possible by the availability of additional genomic information (<a id="xref-fig-1-3" class="xref-fig" href="#F1">Fig. 1</a>). We first used a read mapping strategy for a selection of species with high-coverage data (<em>n</em> = 20; mean = 42.63 Gb; SD = 5.59 Gb) to identify all high-confidence genes in the <em>A. semialata</em> reference genome with a higher percentage identity to one of 17 potential donors [<em>Themeda triandra</em> sequenced here and 16 species from National Center for Biotechnology Information (NCBI) Sequence Read Archive (SRA); <a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1810031116/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, <em>Supplementary Methods</em></a> and <a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1810031116/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw">Dataset S1</a>] than to the three conspecifics or congeners. This initial genome scan identified 1,148 LGT candidates (5.21% of high-confidence protein-coding genes), although these likely contain many false positives, for instance caused by gene losses in the close relatives. We therefore subjected all 1,148 LGT candidates to phylogenetic investigation, using two successive steps, which first identified and subsequently validated well-supported LGT candidates (<a id="xref-fig-1-4" class="xref-fig" href="#F1">Fig. 1</a>).</p><div id="F1" class="fig pos-float type-featured  odd"><div class="highwire-figure"><div class="fig-inline-img-wrapper"><div class="fig-inline-img"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/116/10/4416/F1.large.jpg?width=800&amp;height=600&amp;carousel=1" title="Overview of the analytical pipeline. (A) For each of the steps used to identify lateral gene transfers in the reference genome of A. semialata, the number of candidates retained/discarded is indicated. (B) The purpose of each set of analyses conducted on the unambiguous LGTs is indicated." class="highwire-fragment fragment-images colorbox-load" rel="gallery-fragment-images-942844453" data-figure-caption="&lt;div class=&quot;highwire-markup&quot;&gt;&lt;div xmlns=&quot;http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml&quot;&gt;Overview of the analytical pipeline. (&lt;em&gt;A&lt;/em&gt;) For each of the steps used to identify lateral gene transfers in the reference genome of &lt;em&gt;A. semialata&lt;/em&gt;, the number of candidates retained/discarded is indicated. (&lt;em&gt;B&lt;/em&gt;) The purpose of each set of analyses conducted on the unambiguous LGTs is indicated.&lt;/div&gt;&lt;/div&gt;" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0"><span class="hw-responsive-img"><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image lazyload" alt="Fig. 1." src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7" data-src="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/116/10/4416/F1.medium.gif" width="440" height="163"/><noscript><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image" alt="Fig. 1." src="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/116/10/4416/F1.medium.gif" width="440" height="163"/></noscript></span></a></div></div><ul class="highwire-figure-links inline"><li class="download-fig first"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/116/10/4416/F1.large.jpg?download=true" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-download" title="Download Fig. 1." data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download figure</a></li>

</ul></div><div class="fig-caption" xmlns:xhtml="http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml"><span class="fig-label">Fig. 1.</span> <p id="p-14" class="first-child">Overview of the analytical pipeline. (<em>A</em>) For each of the steps used to identify lateral gene transfers in the reference genome of <em>A. semialata</em>, the number of candidates retained/discarded is indicated. (<em>B</em>) The purpose of each set of analyses conducted on the unambiguous LGTs is indicated.</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div><p id="p-15">First, sequences from the <em>A. semialata</em> genome were compared with those of 16 completely sequenced grass genomes and 20 grass transcriptomes (<a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1810031116/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, <em>Supplementary Methods</em></a> and <a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1810031116/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw">Dataset S1</a>). A coalescence multigene “species” phylogenetic tree was inferred for these 36 taxa and <em>A. semialata</em> using 200 universal single-copy orthologs identified using BUSCO (<a id="xref-ref-46-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-46">46</a>). The 37-taxa species tree was generally well resolved, and overall congruent with previous analyses (<a id="xref-fig-2-1" class="xref-fig" href="#F2">Fig. 2</a>) (<a id="xref-ref-47-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-47">47</a>, <a id="xref-ref-48-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-48">48</a>). While there is some gene tree discordance, particularly for the nodes at the base of the Paniceae, there are well-supported groups spread throughout the phylogenetic tree that are recovered by a majority of the nuclear markers (<a id="xref-fig-2-2" class="xref-fig" href="#F2">Fig. 2</a> and <a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1810031116/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Table S1</a>). Genes of <em>A. semialata</em> positioned within any of these well-supported clades should therefore be considered strong LGT candidates. For each of these 1,148 LGT candidates, we then inferred a maximum likelihood phylogenetic tree using the same 37 taxa. If there was support (&gt;50% bootstrap replicates) for the LGT candidate being nested within (not just sister to) one of the groups retrieved by the coalescence species tree, it was retained for further analysis. We further retained sequences of <em>A. semialata</em> sister to groups represented by a single sequence, such as Melinidinae, and those outside of the core Panicoideae not assigned to a well-supported clade (i.e., <em>Stipagrostis hirtigluma</em>, <em>Danthonia californica</em>, or <em>Chasmanthium latifolium</em>; <a id="xref-fig-2-3" class="xref-fig" href="#F2">Fig. 2</a>) if their combined sister group was as expected based on the species tree (<a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1810031116/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, <em>Supplementary Methods</em></a>). The phylogenetic analyses and topological filtering retained 55 LGT candidates (<a id="xref-fig-1-5" class="xref-fig" href="#F1">Fig. 1</a> and <a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1810031116/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Table S1</a>).</p><div id="F2" class="fig pos-float type-figure  odd"><div class="highwire-figure"><div class="fig-inline-img-wrapper"><div class="fig-inline-img"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/116/10/4416/F2.large.jpg?width=800&amp;height=600&amp;carousel=1" title="Multigene coalescence species tree. The relationships are based on 200 single-copy genes extracted from complete genomes (bold species names) and transcriptomes of 37 grass species. The pie charts show the proportion of quartets supporting the species tree topology (blue) and the two alternative topologies (red and orange, respectively). Posterior probabilities supporting values &#x2265;0.50 are shown near nodes, and branch lengths are in coalescent units, with null terminal branches and dashed lines connecting to species names. The main clades of grasses are delimited on the Right. The position of the A. semialata reference genome is highlighted in yellow, the groups of donors in red, and the clade that contains Themeda is indicated. Red arrows represent the transfers of fragments from each identified donor into A. semialata, with those reported before (32) indicated with blue dashes. And., Andropogoneae; Cench., Cenchrinae; Chlor., Chloridoideae; Melin., Melinidinae; Panici., Panicinae; and Pasp., Paspaleae." class="highwire-fragment fragment-images colorbox-load" rel="gallery-fragment-images-942844453" data-figure-caption="&lt;div class=&quot;highwire-markup&quot;&gt;&lt;div xmlns=&quot;http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml&quot;&gt;Multigene coalescence species tree. <li class="new-tab"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/116/10/4416/F2.large.jpg" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-newtab" target="_blank" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Open in new tab</a></li>

</ul></div><div class="fig-caption"><span class="fig-label">Fig. 2.</span> <p id="p-16" class="first-child">Multigene coalescence species tree. The relationships are based on 200 single-copy genes extracted from complete genomes (bold species names) and transcriptomes of 37 grass species. The pie charts show the proportion of quartets supporting the species tree topology (blue) and the two alternative topologies (red and orange, respectively). Posterior probabilities supporting values ≥0.50 are shown near nodes, and branch lengths are in coalescent units, with null terminal branches and dashed lines connecting to species names. The main clades of grasses are delimited on the <em>Right</em>. The position of the <em>A. semialata</em> reference genome is highlighted in yellow, the groups of donors in red, and the clade that contains <em>Themeda</em> is indicated. Red arrows represent the transfers of fragments from each identified donor into <em>A. semialata</em>, with those reported before (<a id="xref-ref-32-9" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-32">32</a>) indicated with blue dashes. And., Andropogoneae; Cench., Cenchrinae; Chlor., Chloridoideae; Melin., Melinidinae; Panici., Panicinae; and Pasp., Paspaleae.</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div><p id="p-17">Second, we validated these 55 candidates by inferring gene trees with dense phylogenetic sampling using sequence information extracted from 238 genome and transcriptome datasets for 147 grass species (including <em>A. semialata</em>; <a id="xref-fig-3-1" class="xref-fig" href="#F3">Fig. 3</a>, <a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1810031116/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, <em>Supplementary Methods</em></a>, and <a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1810031116/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw">Dataset S2</a>). To allow comparison with the candidate LGT gene tree, a coalescence species tree was inferred for these 147 species with the same 200 BUSCO markers as used above (<a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1810031116/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, <em>Supplementary Methods</em> and Fig. S4</a>). The topology of the species tree recovered the main taxonomic groups that have been identified in the reduced phylogenetic tree (<a id="xref-fig-2-4" class="xref-fig" href="#F2">Fig. 2</a>) and previous published grass phylogenetic trees based on plastids or a few nuclear markers (<a id="xref-ref-47-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-47">47</a>, <a id="xref-ref-48-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-48">48</a>). The 55 candidate LGT gene trees were manually inspected to verify that (<em>i</em>) the positioning of <em>A. semialata</em> sequences within a group of distant relatives was well supported (&gt;70% bootstrap values), and (<em>ii</em>) the number of species represented by different paralogs (identified by comparing the gene and species trees) was sufficient to draw conclusions regarding phylogenetic relationships. In total, five genes were discarded because fewer than three species were represented outside of <em>Alloteropsis</em> and the putative donor clades, or in the donor clade. A further 14 genes were discarded because they inferred relationships that strongly differed from the species tree and were deemed phylogenetically unreliable. In two cases, the <em>A. semialata</em> gene was not nested in a distant clade with the denser sampling, and in six phylogenetic trees, the nesting was not supported by bootstrap values above 70%. The remaining 28 candidates were subjected to further validation to rule out alternative scenarios.</p><div id="F3" class="fig pos-float type-figure  odd"><div class="highwire-figure"><div class="fig-inline-img-wrapper"><div class="fig-inline-img"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/116/10/4416/F3.large.jpg?width=800&amp;height=600&amp;carousel=1" title="Phylogenetic evidence for LGT in the reference A. semialata genome. The gene ASEM_AUS1_12633 from A. semialata was laterally acquired from an Australian T. triandra. The maximum likelihood phylogeny inferred from third positions of codons (Dataset S3) is shown, with the LGT in A. semialata in red and the native orthologs in blue. The region of the phylogeny containing the LGT [red dashed rectangle (Upper) is expanded (Lower)]. Bootstrap support values &#x2265;75% are shown (Lower) or denoted as asterisks (Upper), and the main taxonomic groups are delimited on the Right as in Fig. 2. Chlor., Chloridoideae." class="highwire-fragment fragment-images colorbox-load" rel="gallery-fragment-images-942844453" data-figure-caption="&lt;div class=&quot;highwire-markup&quot;&gt;&lt;div xmlns=&quot;http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml&quot;&gt;Phylogenetic evidence for LGT in the reference &lt;em&gt;A. semialata&lt;/em&gt; genome. Chlor., Chloridoideae.&lt;/div&gt;&lt;/div&gt;" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0"><span class="hw-responsive-img"><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image lazyload" alt="Fig. 3." src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7" data-src="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/116/10/4416/F3.medium.gif" width="259" height="440"/><noscript><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image" alt="Fig. 3." src="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/116/10/4416/F3.medium.gif" width="259" height="440"/></noscript></span></a></div></div><ul class="highwire-figure-links inline"><li class="download-fig first"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/116/10/4416/F3.large.jpg?download=true" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-download" title="Download Fig. 3." data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download figure</a></li>

</ul></div><div class="fig-caption"><span class="fig-label">Fig. 3.</span> <p id="p-18" class="first-child">Phylogenetic evidence for LGT in the reference <em>A. semialata</em> genome. The gene ASEM_AUS1_12633 from <em>A. semialata</em> was laterally acquired from an Australian <em>T. triandra</em>. The maximum likelihood phylogeny inferred from third positions of codons (<a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1810031116/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw">Dataset S3</a>) is shown, with the LGT in <em>A. semialata</em> in red and the native orthologs in blue. The region of the phylogeny containing the LGT [red dashed rectangle (<em>Upper</em>) is expanded (<em>Lower</em>)]. Bootstrap support values ≥75% are shown (<em>Lower</em>) or denoted as asterisks (<em>Upper</em>), and the main taxonomic groups are delimited on the <em>Right</em> as in <a id="xref-fig-2-5" class="xref-fig" href="#F2">Fig. 2</a>. Chlor., Chloridoideae.</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div><p id="p-19">We first demonstrated phylogenetic support for the LGT using approximately unbiased (AU) tests, which confirmed that the topology inferring a LGT was significantly better than a topology indicating no LGT in all but two cases (Bonferroni-corrected <em>P</em> value &lt;0.05; <a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1810031116/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Table S2</a>). Second, phylogenetic biases due to adaptive evolution were ruled out by showing that phylogenetic trees based on third codon positions supported the same groupings for all remaining 26 candidates (<a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1810031116/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw">Dataset S3</a>). Third, unrecognized paralogies were excluded by demonstrating that genes from <em>S. italica</em> and <em>S. bicolor</em> are generally syntenic with the copy of <em>A. semialata</em> positioned as expected based on the species tree (i.e., native copy), but not with the <em>A. semialata</em> copy in a phylogenetic position suggestive of LGT (<a id="xref-fig-4-1" class="xref-fig" href="#F4">Fig. 4</a> and <a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1810031116/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Figs. S2 and S5</a>). Fourth, the existence of the 26 remaining candidates was confirmed by independent sequencing runs from multiple samples, ruling out contamination. The 26 candidates passing all of the tests are considered unambiguous LGTs.</p><div id="F4" class="fig pos-float type-figure  odd"><div class="highwire-figure"><div class="fig-inline-img-wrapper"><div class="fig-inline-img"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/116/10/4416/F4.large.jpg?width=800&amp;height=600&amp;carousel=1" title="Phylogenetic and genomic distribution of LGTs in Alloteropsis. (<em>D</em>) The donors of the 23 LGT fragments, shown with colors and letters as in <em>A</em> and <em>C</em>, are listed with the main group and subgroup, as in <a id="xref-fig-2-6" class="xref-fig" href="#F2">Fig. 2</a> and in <a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1810031116/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Fig. S4</a>.</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div></div><div id="sec-4" class="subsection"><h3>A Minimum of Nine Donors Were Involved in the LGTs.</h3><p id="p-21">For each unambiguous LGT, the putative donor was identified based on the gene trees. Some of the genes could be assigned to precise lineages, such as the <em>Cenchrus</em> genus within Cenchrinae and <em>T. triandra</em> in Andropogoneae (<a id="xref-fig-3-2" class="xref-fig" href="#F3">Figs. 3</a> and <a id="xref-fig-5-1" class="xref-fig" href="#F5">5</a> and <a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1810031116/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw">Dataset S2</a>). Because these two groups were suggested as potential donors of previously reported LGTs in <em>Alloteropsis</em> (<a id="xref-ref-32-10" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-32">32</a>), they were purposely included in the genomic datasets. In the case of <em>Themeda</em>, the inclusion of multiple accessions further identifies the genomic origin of the donor (Australian <em>T. triandra</em> as opposed to African populations; <a id="xref-fig-3-3" class="xref-fig" href="#F3">Fig. 3</a>). When the donor species is not included in one of the available genomic datasets, it cannot be identified to the species level, but can be assigned to one of the higher-level groups supported by the coalescence species tree (<a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1810031116/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Fig. S4</a>), with an accuracy that depends on the resolution of the gene tree and the sampling density in the clade containing the donor. It is therefore possible that events associated with the same higher-level group correspond to different species, which would increase the number of donors. Taking into account only the nonoverlapping groups, the phylogenetic positioning of the unambiguous candidates indicates at least nine different donors from the Andropogoneae, Cenchrinae, Melinidinae, and Chloridoideae groups (<a id="xref-fig-2-7" class="xref-fig" href="#F2">Fig. 2</a> and <a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1810031116/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Fig. S4</a>). These donors diverged from <em>Alloteropsis</em> between 20 and 40 My ago and are separated by multiple speciation events that generated thousands of descending species (<a id="xref-ref-32-11" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-32">32</a>, <a id="xref-ref-47-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-47">47</a>, <a id="xref-ref-48-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-48">48</a>).</p><div id="F5" class="fig pos-float type-figure  odd"><div class="highwire-figure"><div class="fig-inline-img-wrapper"><div class="fig-inline-img"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/116/10/4416/F5.large.jpg?width=800&amp;height=600&amp;carousel=1" title="Phylogenetic tree of Panicoideae genes encoding phosphoenolpyruvate carboxykinase (PCK). The maximum likelihood phylogeny was inferred from gene regions extending from exon 2 to exon 10, and including introns. The three LGTs are highlighted in red, and their corresponding native copies are highlighted in blue. Sequences were either extracted from complete genomes (or transcriptome for Cymbopogon flexuosus) or retrieved from GenBank (accession nos. shown). Bootstrap supports &#x2265;50% are indicated near nodes and the A. semialata reference genome sequences are shown in bold. Branch lengths are given in expected substitutions per site. The main groups are delimited on the Right, as in Fig. 2." class="highwire-fragment fragment-images colorbox-load" rel="gallery-fragment-images-942844453" data-figure-caption="&lt;div class=&quot;highwire-markup&quot;&gt;&lt;div xmlns=&quot;http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml&quot;&gt;Phylogenetic tree of Panicoideae genes encoding phosphoenolpyruvate carboxykinase (PCK). Bootstrap supports &#x2265;50% are indicated near nodes and the &lt;em&gt;A. semialata&lt;/em&gt; reference genome sequences are shown in bold. Branch lengths are given in expected substitutions per site. The main groups are delimited on the &lt;em&gt;Right&lt;/em&gt;, as in Fig. 2.&lt;/div&gt;&lt;/div&gt;" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0"><span class="hw-responsive-img"><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image lazyload" alt="Fig. 5." src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7" data-src="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/116/10/4416/F5.medium.gif" width="357" height="440"/><noscript><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image" alt="Fig. 5." src="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/116/10/4416/F5.medium.gif" width="357" height="440"/></noscript></span></a></div></div><ul class="highwire-figure-links inline"><li class="download-fig first"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/116/10/4416/F5.large.jpg?download=true" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-download" title="Download Fig. 5." data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download figure</a></li>
<li class="new-tab"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/116/10/4416/F5.large.jpg" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-newtab" target="_blank" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Open in new tab</a></li>
<li class="download-ppt last"><a href="/highwire/powerpoint/852344" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-ppt" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download powerpoint</a></li>
</ul></div><div class="fig-caption"><span class="fig-label">Fig. 5.</span> <p id="p-22" class="first-child">Phylogenetic tree of Panicoideae genes encoding phosphoenolpyruvate carboxykinase (PCK). The maximum likelihood phylogeny was inferred from gene regions extending from exon 2 to exon 10, and including introns. The three LGTs are highlighted in red, and their corresponding native copies are highlighted in blue. Sequences were either extracted from complete genomes (or transcriptome for <em>Cymbopogon flexuosus</em>) or retrieved from GenBank (accession nos. shown). Bootstrap supports ≥50% are indicated near nodes and the <em>A. semialata</em> reference genome sequences are shown in bold. Branch lengths are given in expected substitutions per site. The main groups are delimited on the <em>Right</em>, as in <a id="xref-fig-2-8" class="xref-fig" href="#F2">Fig. 2</a>.</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div></div><div id="sec-5" class="subsection"><h3>The Genes Were Transferred as Part of Large Genomic Blocks.</h3><p id="p-23">The locations of the 26 unambiguous LGTs in the reference genome were determined, and the amount of DNA involved in the transfers was assessed by mapping reads of close relatives and putative donors onto the reference genome (<a id="xref-fig-6-1" class="xref-fig" href="#F6">Fig. 6</a>, <a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1810031116/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, <em>Supplementary Methods</em></a>, and <a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1810031116/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw">Dataset S4</a>). These 26 primary LGTs are located on 23 different fragments of foreign DNA distributed throughout the reference genome, including multiple chromosomes (<a id="xref-fig-4-2" class="xref-fig" href="#F4">Fig. 4</a>). We identified protein-coding genes surrounding these primary LGTs and inferred phylogentic trees for them. In total, 26 of the phylogenetic trees built for the surrounding genes supported an LGT scenario involving the same donor as the adjacent primary candidate (<a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1810031116/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Table S3</a> and <a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1810031116/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw">Dataset S5</a>). For a further seven genes surrounding the primary candidates, homologs were present in an insufficient number of species or the gene was truncated and too short to infer well-supported phylogenetic trees. We therefore used a combination of mapping of reads from relatives of the putative donor (<a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1810031116/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw">Dataset S4</a>), BLAST searches, and synteny analyses to support their LGT origin (<a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1810031116/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Table S3</a>), bringing the total of secondary candidates to 33 (<a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1810031116/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, <em>Supplementary Methods</em></a>). Seven of these 33 candidates had been detected by our pipeline, but discarded in the second filter because of a lack of resolution of the trees or low statistical support. The identity of the donor was refined, taking into account all genes in each fragment.</p><div id="F6" class="fig pos-float type-figure  odd"><div class="highwire-figure"><div class="fig-inline-img-wrapper"><div class="fig-inline-img"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/116/10/4416/F6.large.jpg?width=800&amp;height=600&amp;carousel=1" title="Genomic context of LGTs in the A. semialata reference genome. For four LGT fragments, a 0.5-Mb genomic region is shown with high-confidence protein-coding genes indicated at the Top of each panel, in red for primary LGT candidates, orange for secondary LGT candidates, and green for native genes (see Fig. 1 for definitions of primary and secondary LGT candidates). For each fragment, the mapping coverage is shown for the closest relative to the donor in the dataset (in blue), the reference genome AUS1, and three conspecifics or congeners with the three-letter prefix for the A. semialata identifiers based on their country of origin. The coverage is shown on a logarithmic scale, with the dotted red lines indicating the coverage expected for single-copy DNA and the gray lines for the coverage expected for a five-copy DNA segment. Black/blue bars represent mapping quality &#x2265;20, while gray bars have mapping quality " class="highwire-fragment fragment-images colorbox-load" rel="gallery-fragment-images-942844453" data-figure-caption="&lt;div class=&quot;highwire-markup&quot;&gt;&lt;div xmlns=&quot;http://www.w3.org/1999/xhtml&quot;&gt;Genomic context of LGTs in the &lt;em&gt;A. semialata&lt;/em&gt; reference genome. For four LGT fragments, a 0.5-Mb genomic region is shown with high-confidence protein-coding genes indicated at the &lt;em&gt;Top&lt;/em&gt; of each panel, in red for primary LGT candidates, orange for secondary LGT candidates, and green for native genes (see Fig. 1 for definitions of primary and secondary LGT candidates). For each fragment, the mapping coverage is shown for the closest relative to the donor in the dataset (in blue), the reference genome AUS1, and three conspecifics or congeners with the three-letter prefix for the &lt;em&gt;A. semialata&lt;/em&gt; identifiers based on their country of origin. The coverage is shown on a logarithmic scale, with the dotted red lines indicating the coverage expected for single-copy DNA and the gray lines for the coverage expected for a five-copy DNA segment. Black/blue bars represent mapping quality &#x2265;20, while gray bars have mapping quality &lt;20 and include reads that map in multiple locations, indicative of repeats. Valid read alignments have a nucleotide identity of &#x2265;90%. The size of the laterally acquired region is indicated by a black bar at the &lt;em&gt;Top&lt;/em&gt; for fragments A and C, but its delimitation is ambiguous for fragments E and N. See Dataset S4 for details.&lt;/div&gt;&lt;/div&gt;" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0"><span class="hw-responsive-img"><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image lazyload" alt="Fig. 6." src="data:image/gif;base64,R0lGODlhAQABAIAAAAAAAP///yH5BAEAAAAALAAAAAABAAEAAAIBRAA7" data-src="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/116/10/4416/F6.medium.gif" width="440" height="324"/><noscript><img class="highwire-fragment fragment-image" alt="Fig. 6." src="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/116/10/4416/F6.medium.gif" width="440" height="324"/></noscript></span></a></div></div><ul class="highwire-figure-links inline"><li class="download-fig first"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/pnas/116/10/4416/F6.large.jpg?download=true" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-download" title="Download Fig. 6." data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download figure</a></li>
