<li class="download-ppt last"><a href="/highwire/powerpoint/852855" class="highwire-figure-link highwire-figure-link-ppt" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Download powerpoint</a></li>
</ul></div><div class="fig-caption"><span class="fig-label">Fig. 6.</span> <p id="p-24" class="first-child">Genomic context of LGTs in the <em>A. semialata</em> reference genome. For four LGT fragments, a 0.5-Mb genomic region is shown with high-confidence protein-coding genes indicated at the <em>Top</em> of each panel, in red for primary LGT candidates, orange for secondary LGT candidates, and green for native genes (see <a id="xref-fig-1-6" class="xref-fig" href="#F1">Fig. 1</a> for definitions of primary and secondary LGT candidates). For each fragment, the mapping coverage is shown for the closest relative to the donor in the dataset (in blue), the reference genome AUS1, and three conspecifics or congeners with the three-letter prefix for the <em>A. semialata</em> identifiers based on their country of origin. The coverage is shown on a logarithmic scale, with the dotted red lines indicating the coverage expected for single-copy DNA and the gray lines for the coverage expected for a five-copy DNA segment. Black/blue bars represent mapping quality ≥20, while gray bars have mapping quality &lt;20 and include reads that map in multiple locations, indicative of repeats. Valid read alignments have a nucleotide identity of ≥90%. All read lengths are 250 bp except for <em>Iseilema membranaceum</em> (151 bp), <em>Miscanthus sinensis</em> (100 bp), and <em>S. italica</em> (95 bp). The size of the laterally acquired region is indicated by a black bar at the <em>Top</em> for fragments A and C, but its delimitation is ambiguous for fragments E and N. See <a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1810031116/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw">Dataset S4</a> for details.</p><div class="sb-div caption-clear"></div></div></div><p id="p-25">Besides multiple protein-coding genes, some of the identified LGT fragments contain long stretches of noncoding DNA with identity to the putative donors above 90% (approximate cutoff in identity for read mapping; <a id="xref-fig-6-2" class="xref-fig" href="#F6">Fig. 6</a> and <a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1810031116/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw">Dataset S4</a>). In the case of recent LGTs from donors closely related to those included in our dataset, the fragments of laterally acquired DNA could be delimited with confidence and were up to 169,972 bp long with &gt;99% identical coding regions (e.g., for the two genes in LGT fragment A), and highly similar intergenic regions (e.g., 97.2% identical over 45.7 kb in fragment A; <a id="xref-fig-6-3" class="xref-fig" href="#F6">Fig. 6</a>). Identifying laterally acquired noncoding DNA is difficult for fragments acquired a long time ago or for which close relatives of the donor have not been sampled, but some of them could be much larger (e.g., fragment N in <a id="xref-fig-6-4" class="xref-fig" href="#F6">Fig. 6</a>), while others are limited to one protein-coding gene and at most a small amount of flanking DNA (fragment E in <a id="xref-fig-6-5" class="xref-fig" href="#F6">Fig. 6</a>).</p><p id="p-26">The number of protein-coding genes contained within the laterally acquired fragments ranged from 1 (13 fragments) to 10 (in fragment C) (<a id="xref-fig-4-3" class="xref-fig" href="#F4">Fig. 4</a>). When multiple genes were identified in a laterally acquired fragment, the genomic segment shows evidence of synteny with the genomes of the two other Panicoideae investigated (<a id="xref-fig-4-4" class="xref-fig" href="#F4">Fig. 4</a> and <a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1810031116/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Fig. S2 and Table S3</a>). We stress, however, that the native and laterally acquired genes of <em>A. semialata</em> are always found in different parts of the genome, with almost all native copies being syntenic with the position of the ortholog in the <em>S. italica</em> and <em>S. bicolor</em> genomes (<a id="xref-fig-4-5" class="xref-fig" href="#F4">Fig. 4</a> and <a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1810031116/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Figs. S2 and S5</a>). By contrast, the LGT are not syntenic with orthologs from <em>S. italica</em> and <em>S. bicolor</em>, with one exception (fragment O; <a id="xref-fig-4-6" class="xref-fig" href="#F4">Fig. 4</a> and <a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1810031116/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Figs. S2 and S5</a>).</p></div><div id="sec-6" class="subsection"><h3>Transposable Elements Were also Transferred and Later Duplicated.</h3><p id="p-27">We assembled a partial genome of <em>T. triandra</em> and identified <em>T. triandra</em> and <em>A. semialata</em> TEs in their respective genome assemblies (<a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1810031116/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, <em>Supplementary Methods</em></a>). In total, 186,270 TEs were annotated in the <em>A. semialata</em> reference genome, accounting for 51% of the assembly (0.39 Gb; <a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1810031116/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Table S4</a>). As expected, they appear prevalent near centromeres (<a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1810031116/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Fig. S3</a>). The TE sequences were then clustered and phylogenetic trees inferred. Combined with coverage analyses, these led to the identification of 92 TEs acquired from <em>T. triandra</em> in the <em>A. semialata</em> genome (<a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1810031116/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, <em>Supplementary Methods</em></a>). Several of these TEs are located within the two fragments acquired from <em>T. triandra</em> containing protein-coding genes and potentially have daughter copies located in other genomic locations (<a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1810031116/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Fig. S3</a>).</p></div><div id="sec-7" class="subsection"><h3>LGTs Happened at Different Times During <em>Alloteropsis</em> Diversification.</h3><p id="p-28">Using resequencing (∼10× coverage) and high-coverage (∼40× coverage) data for an additional 24 <em>Alloteropsis</em> populations representing the genetic diversity in this species (<a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1810031116/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Fig. S4</a>), we were able to establish the distribution of the LGTs across the <em>Alloteropsis</em> phylogenetic tree, thereby estimating the relative timing of the transfers (<a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1810031116/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, <em>Supplementary Methods</em></a>). Fragment H was unique to the Australian reference genome, and four other fragments (A–D) are restricted to the other Australian accession (AUS2) as well as the accession from the Philippines (PHI1), indicating recent acquisitions. The other LGTs were also observed in a number of individuals from <em>A. semialata</em> and/or the sister species <em>Alloteropsis angusta</em> (<a id="xref-fig-4-7" class="xref-fig" href="#F4">Fig. 4</a>). Some are present in most <em>A. semialata</em> accessions (T, I, F, and G), suggesting an early acquisition followed by retention of the genes. Others are distributed among more distantly related accessions, suggesting early acquisitions followed by losses or introgression among <em>A. semialata</em> populations after the transfer (<a id="xref-fig-4-8" class="xref-fig" href="#F4">Fig. 4</a>).</p></div><div id="sec-8" class="subsection"><h3>LGTs Added Functional Diversity to the Recipient Genomes.</h3><p id="p-29">RNA-Seq data for 16 populations of <em>A. semialata</em> and one of the sister species <em>A. angusta</em> were mapped to the coding sequences from the <em>A. semialata</em> reference genome, and the expression levels of LGTs and their native orthologs were estimated in leaf and root samples (<a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1810031116/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, <em>Supplementary Methods</em></a>). Over 59% (35 out of 59) of the primary and secondary LGTs were expressed [&gt;1 reads per kilobase of transcript per million mapped reads (rpkm)] in at least one population (<a id="xref-fig-4-9" class="xref-fig" href="#F4">Fig. 4</a> and <a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1810031116/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw">Dataset S1</a>). The mean expression level of 12 laterally acquired genes was higher than their respective native homolog in at least one <em>A. semialata</em> population (<a id="xref-fig-4-10" class="xref-fig" href="#F4">Fig. 4</a> and <a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1810031116/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw">Dataset S1</a>), and in one example the expression of the native ortholog seems to have been replaced by the LGT (ASEM_AUS1_12633; <a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1810031116/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw">Dataset S1</a>). The functions of the laterally acquired genes include those known to be involved in C<sub>4</sub> photosynthesis as well as loci associated with disease resistance and abiotic stress tolerance (<a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1810031116/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Table S5</a>).</p></div><div id="sec-9" class="subsection"><h3>LGTs Involving Other Recipient Grasses Are Detected.</h3><p id="p-30">Our phylogenetic trees suggest LGTs involving recipients other than <em>A. semialata</em>. Indeed, the visual inspection of the gene trees for primary LGTs including the 147 species identified 10 genes with bootstrap support for positions suggestive of LGT in grasses other than the reference genome (<a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1810031116/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Table S6</a> and <a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1810031116/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw">Dataset S2</a>). For three of them, the lack of sequencing replicates for these samples generated in other projects meant that contaminations could not be ruled out (<a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1810031116/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Table S6</a>). However, seven genes from individuals other than the reference genome were statistically supported in a position suggestive of LGT by AU tests (Bonferroni-corrected <em>P</em> value &lt;0.05; <a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1810031116/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Table S7</a>), and this conclusion was supported by replicate datasets (<a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1810031116/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Table S6</a>). This included two genes encoding a C<sub>4</sub> enzyme that had been previously identified in some non-Australian populations of <em>A. semialata</em> (<a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1810031116/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw">Dataset S2</a>) (<a id="xref-ref-32-12" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-32">32</a>, <a id="xref-ref-36-4" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-36">36</a>). In addition we identified LGTs in five other Panicoideae genomes (<a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1810031116/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Table S6</a>).</p><p id="p-31">For two genes containing non-<em>A. semialata</em> LGTs we had sufficient genome data for the recipient species to assemble full-length gene sequences. These data were supplemented with similar full-length sequences from published genomes and the NCBI nucleotide database to infer gene trees using intron and exon sequences. This confirmed that the gene encoding phosphoenolpyruvate carbokykinase (PCK; ASEM_AUS1_17510 on fragment L), an enzyme involved in some subtypes of C<sub>4</sub> photosynthesis (<a id="xref-ref-49-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-49">49</a>), was laterally acquired by <em>A. semialata</em>, <em>Echinochloa</em>, and <em>Cymbopogon</em> (<a id="xref-fig-5-2" class="xref-fig" href="#F5">Fig. 5</a>). The three LGTs of PCK, supported by multiple datasets, did not form a monophyletic group, suggesting they result from independent transfer events (<a id="xref-fig-5-3" class="xref-fig" href="#F5">Fig. 5</a>). Transcriptome datasets for these species indicate that both <em>Cymbopogon</em> and <em>Echinochloa</em> express the LGTs in their leaves, where they likely play a role in their C<sub>4</sub> photosynthetic pathway (<a id="xref-ref-50-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-50">50</a>, <a id="xref-ref-51-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-51">51</a>). Despite the smaller sample size, the phylogenetic tree inferred from sequences homologous to ASEM_AUS1_20550 similarly confirmed the LGT scenario for this gene observed in <em>Alloteropsis cimicina</em> (<a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1810031116/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Fig. S6</a>). For ASEM_AUS1_20550 the donor for the LGT detected in the reference genome belonged to the Andropogoneae, whereas the gene detected in <em>A. cimicina</em> was acquired from a Cenchrinae (<a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1810031116/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Fig. S6</a>). The native copy of <em>A. cimicina</em> was also detected, and transcriptome data show that the LGT is expressed at a higher level than the native copy.</p></div></div><div class="section discussion" id="sec-10"><h2 class="">Discussion</h2><div id="sec-11" class="subsection"><h3>Multiple Laterally Acquired Genes in the Genome of <em>A. semialata</em>.</h3><p id="p-32">Using a combination of stringent phylogenetic and genomic analyses (<a id="xref-fig-1-7" class="xref-fig" href="#F1">Fig. 1</a>), we identify 59 genes in the genome of the grass <em>A. semialata</em> that were laterally acquired from other grasses. Our pipeline was designed to rule out the three main alternative explanations to LGT, namely: (<em>i</em>) unrecognized parology by comparing synteny with other grass genomes, (<em>ii</em>) contamination by having independent sequencing supporting the existence of the genes, and (<em>iii</em>) convergent evolution by using different data partitions. In addition, long reads spanning the laterally acquired and native DNA prove that the fragments are integrated in the genome of <em>A. semialata</em> (<a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1810031116/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Fig. S7</a>). The LGTs are moreover supported a posteriori by our genomic analyses, which show that half of the primary candidates are flanked by coding, and in some cases noncoding, regions with a high similarity to the same putative donors (<a id="xref-fig-6-6" class="xref-fig" href="#F6">Fig. 6</a> and <a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1810031116/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw">Dataset S4</a>). The 10 fragments that contain multiple protein-coding genes represent the most unequivocal cases of LGTs and demonstrate that large stretches of DNA containing numerous genes can be transferred among distantly related grass species.</p><p id="p-33">The number of LGTs reported here is likely an underestimate. For example, LGTs that lack phylogenetic informativeness because the genes are too short, or are not present in a sufficient number of grass species, would be excluded by our analysis. More importantly, we focused on gene transfers among distant relatives to differentiate LGTs from other processes that can create discordance between gene and species trees, such as incomplete lineage sorting and hybridization (<a id="xref-fig-2-9" class="xref-fig" href="#F2">Fig. 2</a>). Similarly, we focused on relatively recent LGTs that have accumulated during the diversification of the <em>Alloteropsis</em> genus, because ancient LGT would alter deep branching patterns that can be detected only when the donor and recipient are extremely distant [e.g., ferns and mosses (<a id="xref-ref-33-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-33">33</a>)]. As a direct consequence of the methodology, all transfers involving donors that are part of poorly resolved clades within the Paniceae tribe (<a id="xref-fig-2-10" class="xref-fig" href="#F2">Fig. 2</a>) or transfers that happened before the diversification of <em>Alloteropsis</em> would remain undetected. In addition, our power to detect LGTs depends directly on the availability of genomic data for close relatives of the donor, particularly when it comes to accurately determining the size of the acquired DNA fragment (<a id="xref-fig-6-7" class="xref-fig" href="#F6">Fig. 6</a>). Thus, the 59 LGTs reported here could be only a subset of those existing in the genome of <em>A. semialata</em>.</p><p id="p-34">In total, the detected LGTs belong to 23 genomic DNA fragments (<a id="xref-fig-4-11" class="xref-fig" href="#F4">Fig. 4</a>). These fragments were laterally acquired from at least nine different grass donors, although the number might be higher if grasses from the same lineage independently provided LGTs (<a id="xref-fig-4-12" class="xref-fig" href="#F4">Fig. 4</a>). Using genomic datasets from multiple <em>Alloteropsis</em> populations allowed us to establish the distribution of each of these fragments within the species. Inferring the presence of a gene from resequencing data can be problematic if the gene is truncated, or located in regions of the genome with reduced sequencing depth. Based on our estimates, fragment H is unique to the reference genome and likely represents the most recent acquisition. Several fragments (A–D) were likely acquired around the time <em>A. semialata</em> colonized Australia, as they are restricted to Australian and Filipino accessions, with the latter probably a result of recent admixture from Australia (<a id="xref-fig-4-13" class="xref-fig" href="#F4">Fig. 4</a>). Other LGT fragments are shared by a majority of <em>A. semialata</em> accessions and were likely acquired near the origin of this species ∼2 My ago (e.g., fragments T and F; <a id="xref-fig-4-14" class="xref-fig" href="#F4">Fig. 4</a>) (<a id="xref-ref-36-5" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-36">36</a>, <a id="xref-ref-41-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-41">41</a>). In some cases, the patchy distribution of LGT fragments could be due to secondary losses after ancient acquisitions, as supported by observed genetic variation among the different <em>A. semialata</em> populations that has likely accumulated since the LGT was acquired (e.g., fragment E; <a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1810031116/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw">Dataset S2</a>). In other cases low levels of genetic variation among accessions of <em>A. semialata</em> and <em>A. angusta</em> suggest that the patchy distribution results from more recent acquisition followed by introgression into different populations (e.g., fragments K and N; <a id="xref-fig-4-15" class="xref-fig" href="#F4">Figs. 4</a> and <a id="xref-fig-6-8" class="xref-fig" href="#F6">6</a> and <a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1810031116/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw">Datasets S2</a> and <a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1810031116/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw">S5</a>) (<a id="xref-ref-52-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-52">52</a>). Overall, the evidence of fragments being acquired at different points suggests that the diversification of <em>Alloteropsis</em> has been punctuated by repeated bouts of LGT (<a id="xref-fig-4-16" class="xref-fig" href="#F4">Fig. 4<em>A</em></a>).</p></div><div id="sec-12" class="subsection"><h3>Transfers of Large DNA Blocks Spread Functional Genes.</h3><p id="p-35">The 23 laterally acquired fragments are widely distributed across the genome of <em>A. semialata</em> (<a id="xref-fig-4-17" class="xref-fig" href="#F4">Fig. 4<em>C</em></a>). While divergence of noncoding DNA hampers a precise delimitation of the acquired fragments in more ancient LGTs, or those for which genome data of a close relative of the donor are missing (e.g., fragments E and N in <a id="xref-fig-6-9" class="xref-fig" href="#F6">Fig. 6</a>), recent LGTs with a sampled donor can be shown to be at least 170 kb long and are composed of genic as well as noncoding regions (e.g., fragments A and C in <a id="xref-fig-6-10" class="xref-fig" href="#F6">Fig. 6</a>). In the case of two fragments acquired from <em>T. triandra</em> (A and B), TE phylogenetic trees and inferences of their recent activity show that some TEs, which were acquired as part of the large DNA fragments, have subsequently transposed to new regions (<a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1810031116/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Fig. S3<em>B</em></a>). The laterally acquired fragments also have TEs specific to <em>Alloteropsis</em>, which were likely inserted after the acquisition, highlighting rearrangements between the native and foreign parts of the genome. Other laterally acquired TEs were detected around the genome outside of the large block of DNA and might represent TEs that escaped from large DNA blocks or elements that were acquired on their own.</p><p id="p-36">Genomic rearrangements that happened after the transfer are also visible in the gene content of the laterally acquired fragments. Erosion is evidenced by gene loss in some accessions (e.g., part of fragment C in one of the Australian samples; <a id="xref-fig-4-18" class="xref-fig" href="#F4">Figs. 4</a> and <a id="xref-fig-6-11" class="xref-fig" href="#F6">6</a>), as well as pseudogenizing mutations in others (<a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1810031116/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Fig. S8</a>). However, 59% of the laterally acquired genes are expressed in at least one population under the conditions evaluated (<a id="xref-fig-4-19" class="xref-fig" href="#F4">Fig. 4</a>). In all cases, the donor and the recipient evolved independently for tens of millions of years before the transfers, leaving ample time for adaptive evolution and functional diversification. The genetic exchanges therefore potentially brought in genes for novel attributes. Eight of the fragments contain at least one gene that is either novel, has replaced the function of the native copy that became a pseudogene, or is expressed at a higher level than the native copy (<a id="xref-fig-4-20" class="xref-fig" href="#F4">Fig. 4</a> and <a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1810031116/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw">Dataset S1</a>). The functional LGTs have therefore added novelty to the genetic apparatus of <em>Alloteropsis</em>, including a variety of disease resistance and abiotic stress response loci (<a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1810031116/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Table S5</a>), in addition to the previously reported photosynthetic genes (<a id="xref-ref-32-13" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-32">32</a>). The advantage of each of the laterally acquired genes is yet to be determined by targeted functional studies, but selection for the novel functions is likely responsible for the retention of LGTs through time (<a id="xref-fig-4-21" class="xref-fig" href="#F4">Fig. 4</a>). Genes that underwent adaptive shifts in some subgroups of grasses might be especially likely to be retained after transfer to other groups, potentially contributing to the three independent LGTs for the C<sub>4</sub> enzyme PCK (<a id="xref-fig-5-4" class="xref-fig" href="#F5">Fig. 5</a>).</p></div><div id="sec-13" class="subsection"><h3>Different Processes Might Have Transferred the Genes.</h3><p id="p-37">The mechanisms underlying the reported grass-to-grass LGTs remain elusive and might vary between events. The nonsyntenic localization of LGTs and their coexistence with native copies argues against classical introgression involving sexual reproduction and chromosomal recombination. The movement of genes could have occurred among genomes following the transient cohabitation of chromosomes from different species within the same nucleus, for example in allopolyploids that can subsequently backcross with diploids (<a id="xref-ref-53-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-53">53</a>). In grasses, the growth of pollen tubes on the stigma of distant relatives can be used experimentally to trigger embryo development with only occasional transfer of paternal DNA (<a id="xref-ref-54-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-54">54</a>, <a id="xref-ref-55-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-55">55</a>), providing a more likely mechanism for chromosomal exchanges. Occasional interspecific cell-to-cell contact could also occur through root-to-root interactions between grasses growing in multispecies clumps as observed in savannas or among pollen tubes of different species growing on the same stigma. Cell-to-cell contacts are known to allow movement of DNA across distinct nuclei in grafts (<a id="xref-ref-56-1" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-56">56</a>) or host/parasite interactions (<a id="xref-ref-22-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-22">22</a><a id="xref-ref-23-2" class="xref-down-link" href="#ref-23"><span>⇓</span></a><a id="xref-ref-24-2" class="xref-down-link" href="#ref-24"><span>⇓</span></a><a id="xref-ref-25-2" class="xref-down-link" href="#ref-25"><span>⇓</span></a><a id="xref-ref-26-2" class="xref-down-link" href="#ref-26"><span>⇓</span></a><a id="xref-ref-27-2" class="xref-down-link" href="#ref-27"><span>⇓</span></a><a id="xref-ref-28-2" class="xref-down-link" href="#ref-28"><span>⇓</span></a><a id="xref-ref-29-3" class="xref-down-link" href="#ref-29"><span>⇓</span></a>–<a id="xref-ref-30-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-30">30</a>). Since <em>A. semialata</em> can propagate vegetatively via rhizomes, both transfers into the seeds and into parts of the root system would allow the long-term integration of LGTs into the germline. Independently of the exact mechanism, our genomic investigations show that genes are recurrently passed among distant species of grasses, and provide a novel source of genetic variation for selection to act upon.</p><p id="p-38">While we screened for nonangiosperm donors, all of the detected LGTs came from grasses. This bias in the donor identity might represent some genomic or physical incompatibilities (e.g., lack of wind dispersal of pollen or different root architecture) that limited exchanges with nongrass angiosperms. All grass donors were from the subfamily Panicoideae, with one exception. Within Panicoideae, two groups (Andropogoneae and Cenchrinae) have contributed the vast majority of fragments, while other groups with similar genomic resources (e.g., Panicinae and Paspaleae; <a id="xref-fig-2-11" class="xref-fig" href="#F2">Fig. 2</a> and <a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1810031116/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Table S1</a>) were not involved in any of the detected LGTs. This bias can largely be explained by geographic patterns, as both Andropogoneae and Cenchrinae species frequently co-occur with <em>A. semialata</em> in large populations throughout Africa, Asia, and Australia, whereas in contrast, Paspaleae species are mainly found in South America where <em>A. semialata</em> does not occur. Whether other groups (e.g., Panicinae) had opportunities to exchange genes with <em>Alloteropsis</em> remains to be formally assessed and may ultimately contribute to identifying the properties that promote LGT.</p></div></div><div class="section conclusions" id="sec-14"><h2 class="">Conclusions</h2><p id="p-39" class="flushleft">Using genomic tools and stringent phylogenetic criteria, we have shown that the genome of an Australian <em>A. semialata</em> individual contains at least 59 genes laterally acquired from a minimum of nine different donors (<a id="xref-fig-2-12" class="xref-fig" href="#F2">Figs. 2</a> and <a id="xref-fig-4-22" class="xref-fig" href="#F4">4</a>). Large-scale pollen dispersal and vegetative growth, which might facilitate cell-to-cell contacts and subsequent LGTs, are widespread among perennial grasses, and LGT is therefore likely to be frequent in this group. Transfer of specific segments of noncoding DNA among members of the grass family has previously been reported (<a id="xref-ref-24-3" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-24">24</a>, <a id="xref-ref-34-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-34">34</a>, <a id="xref-ref-35-2" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-35">35</a>), but the widespread transfer of functional genes documented here shows that this process is likely to have consequences for adaptation. We also detect functional LGTs among grass species other than <em>Alloteropsis</em>, with recipients in the genera <em>Cymbopogon</em>, <em>Danthoniopsis</em>, <em>Echinochloa</em>, and <em>Oplismenus</em> (<a id="xref-fig-5-5" class="xref-fig" href="#F5">Fig. 5</a>, <a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1810031116/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, Fig. S6 and Table S6</a>, and <a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1810031116/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw">Dataset S2</a>). While these other instances need to be investigated with dedicated genomic work, they do show that <em>A. semialata</em> is not exceptional in this group of eukaryotes that might exhibit something approaching a type of pangenome. In particular, future efforts should determine whether the process is pervasive in the family, or whether it is restricted to certain growth forms or ecological types. The evidence presented here already shows that the widespread transfer of functional genetic elements reported for <em>Alloteropsis</em> might be just the tip of the iceberg, with functional LGTs among grasses, and potentially other groups of plants, having remained undetected because of limited taxon sampling and a lack of dedicated searches. We conclude that LGT might constitute an underappreciated contributor to the functional diversification of some groups of plants, which can act as a source of genetic variation, potentially of adaptive significance.</p></div><div class="section materials-methods" id="sec-15"><h2 class="">Materials and Methods</h2><p id="p-40" class="flushleft">This section gives a summary of the extensive methodology, which is detailed in <a href="https://www.pnas.org/lookup/suppl/doi:10.1073/pnas.1810031116/-/DCSupplemental" class="in-nw"><em>SI Appendix</em>, <em>Supplementary Methods</em></a>. In short, a chromosome-level reference genome was generated for a single Australian plant of <em>A. semialata</em>, selected because it was previously shown to contain a gene laterally acquired from a member of the <em>Themeda</em> genus (<a id="xref-ref-32-14" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-32">32</a>, <a id="xref-ref-36-6" class="xref-bibr" href="#ref-36">36</a>). Ab initio gene prediction was used to annotate the reference genome using a combination of <em>A. semialata</em> transcriptome data and protein sequences from model Panicoideae species (<em>S. italica</em> and <em>S. bicolor</em>). A combination of similarity and phylogenetic analyses were used to identify unambiguous LGT among these genes (<a id="xref-fig-1-8" class="xref-fig" href="#F1">Fig. 1</a>).</p><p id="p-41">We first used high-coverage Illumina genome data (∼40 Gbp per sample) to scan the Australian <em>A. semialata</em> genome for loci that are more similar to a potential donor species than to a close relative, representing LGT candidates (<a id="xref-fig-1-9" class="xref-fig" href="#F1">Fig. 1</a>). Datasets for three close relatives were used to capture LGTs that occurred at different time points during the diversification of <em>Alloteropsis</em>, and equivalent data were generated or retrieved from the literature for 17 other species, including <em>Themeda</em>, representing potential donors distributed across the grass family.</p><p id="p-42">All candidates from the initial genome-wide scan were subsequently verified using phylogenetic trees. Up to 147 grass species were included and genes were considered to be laterally acquired if they fulfilled a number of stringent criteria (<a id="xref-fig-1-10" class="xref-fig" href="#F1">Fig. 1</a>). The genomic fragments containing the detected LGTs were characterized, and the potential adaptive significance of the LGTs was assessed using RNA-Seq data and functional annotation. Finally, different classes of TEs were annotated using existing pipelines, and phylogenetic trees combined with coverage analyses were used to identify those TEs transferred from <em>T. triandra</em> to <em>A. semialata</em>.</p></div><div class="section ack" id="ack-1"><h2>Acknowledgments</h2><p id="p-43">We thank Tricia Handasyde (Department of Parks and Wildlife, Western Australia) for providing samples.     <div class="node--featured-item__links"><span><a href="http://feeds.feedburner.com/pnas/uSDM" target="_blank" class="" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Past Podcasts</a></span><span><a href="https://itunes.apple.com/us/podcast/pnas-science-sessions/id1367460245" target="_blank" class="" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Subscribe</a></span></div>
    
  </div> <!-- End of body -->

</article>
  </div>
  <div class="views-row-odd views-row-last">
    <article class="node-view-teaser node-featured-item">

    <div class="node--image">
    <a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/117/39/24195" class="" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0"><div class="field field-name-field-featured-item-image field-type-image field-label-hidden"><div class="field-items"><div class="field-item even"><img src="https://www.pnas.org/sites/default/files/styles/medium/public/additional-assets/featured-item/pitn-1-01-11-220.jpg?itok=uHvXa70H" width="220" height="220" alt="Goats standing in a pin" /></div></div></div></a>  </div> <!-- End of image -->
  
  <div class="node--body">

    
        <div class="node--featured-item__title"><a href="https://www.pnas.org/content/117/39/24195" class="" data-icon-position="" data-hide-link-title="0">Transplantation of sperm-producing stem cells</a></div>
    

        <div class="node--featured-item__description">CRISPR-Cas9 gene editing can improve the effectiveness of spermatogonial stem cell transplantation in mice and livestock, a study finds.</div>
    

        <div class="node--featured-item__credits text-small">Image credit: Jon M. Oatley.</div>
    
    
  </div> <!-- End of body -->

</article>
